Abu Yusuf's Principle for Walking and Riding in Ramy
Anecdote of Abu Yusuf teaching the principle: every ramy with subsequent ramy walk, final ramy ride.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the mode of ramy (walking vs riding) and explain the principle distinguishing the first jamarat from the final one.

Core Idea
For ramy: walk for jamarat that precede another, ride for the final one.

This lesson presents a remarkable anecdote from the deathbed of Imam Abu Yusuf, one of the foremost Hanafi jurists. Despite his critical condition, he was eager to correct a common misconception about the performance of ramy (pelting the jamarat). Through this story, we learn a precise legal principle that distinguishes the mode of walking or riding based on the sequence of the jamarat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The dispute is about whether the pilgrim should walk or ride while pelting each of the three jamarat (stone pillars) during Hajj.
2
Understand the common assumption
Ibn al-Jarrah initially assumed the pilgrim should always walk. Abu Yusuf corrected him twice, showing that a single answer is wrong.
This highlights that the ruling depends on context.
3
State Abu Yusuf's principle
Abu Yusuf's answer: 'Every pelting after which there is a pelting, he pelts walking; every pelting after which there is no pelting, he pelts riding.'
The first two jamarat have subsequent jamarat; the final one does not.
4
Apply the principle
On the 11th and 12th of Dhul-Hijjah, the pilgrim pelts three jamarat. For the first two (al-Ula and al-Wusta), walk; for the third (al-Aqabah), it is mustahab to ride. On the 13th, if staying, only the third is pelted, so ride for that one as well.
Walking and riding are both permissible, but this is the preferred method.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some narrations suggest always walking or always riding; but the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Yusuf's distinction.
This is an example of tarjih within the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأول مروي عن أبي يوسف ، فإنه قد ذكر ابن الجراح - وهو من أكبر تلامذة عطاء بن أبي رباح تلميذ ابن عباس ما وكان عالما بالمناسك - أنه قال: دخلت على أبي يوسف وقد أغمي عليه فأفاق فلما رآني قال: يا إبراهيم ما تقول في رمي الجمار يرميها الحاج راكبا أو ماشيا؟ فقلت يرميها ماشيا فقال: أخطأت، فقلت: يرميها راكبا، فقال: أخطأت قلت فماذا يقول الإمام، قال: كل رمي بعده رمي، يرميها ماشيا، وكل رمي ليس بعده رمي، يرميها راكبا فخرجت من عنده فسمعت بكاء الناس في داره فقيل لي قضي أبو يوسف له، فتعجبت من حرصه على العلم في مثل هذه الحادثة
The first is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Ibn al-Jarrah – who was one of the greatest students of Ata' ibn Abi Rabah, a student of Ibn Abbas, and was knowledgeable in the rituals – said: I entered upon Abu Yusuf while he had fainted. Then he regained consciousness and when he saw me he said: O Ibrahim, what do you say about the pelting of the jamarat: does the pilgrim pelt them riding or walking? I said: He pelts them walking. He said: You are wrong. I said: He pelts them riding. He said: You are wrong. I said: Then what does the Imam say? He said: Every pelting after which there is a pelting, he pelts them walking, and every pelting after which there is no pelting, he pelts them riding. I left from his presence and heard the weeping of the people in his house. It was said to me: Abu Yusuf has passed away. I was amazed at his eagerness for knowledge in such a situation.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute story that clarifies the ruling on the mode of ramy. The author of Nur al-Idah and the commentator (from Tabayyun al-Haqa'iq) narrate this incident to explain the preferred method. Abu Yusuf, the great Hanafi jurist, on his deathbed corrected the famous scholar Ibn al-Jarrah who thought the pilgrim should always walk for ramy. Abu Yusuf taught that the principle is: for any jamrah after which another jamrah will be pelted (like the first two on the 11th and 12th), one should walk; for the final jamrah (Jamrat al-Aqabah or the last one on the final day) where no subsequent pelting follows, one should ride. This shows his dedication to teaching precise rulings even at the moment of death.

Key Terms
رمي الجمار (ramy al-jimar): Pelting the three stone pillars at Mina with pebbles.
رمي بعده رمي (ramy ba'dahu ramy): A pelting after which there is another pelting (i.e., a jamrah that is not the last in the day's sequence).
رمي ليس بعده رمي (ramy laysa ba'dahu ramy): A pelting after which there is no further pelting (i.e., the final jamrah of the day).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the story from Abu Yusuf, how should the final jamrah (Jamrat al-Aqabah) be pelted?
A) Walking without supplication
B) Riding without supplication
C) Walking with supplication
D) Riding with supplication

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's principle: walk for jamarat that are followed by another jamrah, ride for the final one without a following jamrah.

Ruling on Staying Overnight in Mina
Ruling on staying in Mina on ramy nights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that staying overnight in Mina during the nights of ramy is the sunnah, and staying elsewhere without excuse is makruh tanzihi with no penalty.

Core Idea
Staying in Mina on ramy nights is sunnah; leaving without excuse is makruh but requires no compensation.

In the final days of Hajj, pilgrims spend three nights in Mina — the 11th, 12th, and if delayed, the 13th of Dhul Hijjah. This passage clarifies that staying anywhere else during these nights is disliked (makruh) but not sinful enough to require a sacrifice. The Prophet ﷺ modeled this practice, and the Hanafi school follows his example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the practice
Read the text: 'وكره المبيت بغير منى ليالي الرمي' — staying overnight in other than Mina during the nights of ramy is makruh. Recognise that the desired practice is to stay in Mina.
The default is to be in Mina; deviation is disliked.
2
Understand the ruling level
'Makruh' here means makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked, not sinful). It does not invalidate Hajj nor require a penalty (dam). Distinguish it from makruh tahrimi which is sinful.
Hanafi usul: makruh tanzihi is close to halal.
3
Apply the ruling
Consider a pilgrim who stays in a hotel outside Mina during these nights. The ruling: it is makruh tanzihi, but his Hajj remains valid and no fidyah is due.
An excuse (illness, lack of space) may lift the dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره المبيت بغير منى ليالي الرمي، ثم إذا رحل إلى مكة نزل بالمحصب ساعة، ثم يدخل مكة، ويطوف بالبيت سبعة أشواط بلا رمل وسعي إن قدّمهما، وهذا طواف الوداع، ويسمى أيضا طواف الصدر، وهذا واجب إلا على أهل مكة ومن أقام بها، ويصلي بعده ركعتين
And it is makruh to stay overnight in other than Mina during the nights of ramy. Then when he departs to Mecca, he stops at al-Muhassab for an hour, then enters Mecca and performs tawaf of the House seven circuits without rapid walking (ramal) or running (sa'y) if he had already done them. This is Tawaf al-Wada', also called Tawaf al-Sadr. This is wajib except for the people of Mecca and those residing there. He then prays two rakats after it.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage covers two issues: (1) Staying overnight during the nights of ramy (11th, 12th, and if needed 13th) should be done in Mina. It is makruh tanzihi to stay elsewhere without excuse, but no penalty. (2) After finishing ramy and departing Mina, the pilgrim stops at al-Muhassab (a place between Mecca and Mina) for some time, then enters Mecca to perform Tawaf al-Wada' (farewell tawaf). This tawaf is performed without ramal (rapid walking) or sa'y if those were already done in Tawaf al-Qudum. It is also called Tawaf al-Sadr. This tawaf is wajib for every pilgrim except those who are from Mecca or have taken up residence there. After the tawaf, two rakats are prayed.

Key Terms
ليالي الرمي (layali al-ramy): The nights of ramy: the 11th, 12th, and if delayed, the 13th of Dhul Hijjah when pilgrims throw pebbles at the Jamarat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on staying overnight outside Mina during the nights of ramy without a valid excuse?
A) Wajib to stay in Mina; leaving requires dam.
B) Makruh tanzihi; no penalty.
C) Haram; Hajj invalid.
D) Sunnah muakkadah; leaving breaks sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Staying overnight in Mina during ramy nights is preferred; staying elsewhere without excuse is only makruh tanzihi and incurs no penalty.

Ruling on Tawaf al-Wada' (Farewell Tawaf)
Ruling on Tawaf al-Wada' obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that Tawaf al-Wada' is wajib for all pilgrims except Meccans and residents, and explain the consequence of its omission.

Core Idea
Tawaf al-Wada' is wajib; omission requires a sacrifice, but Meccans and residents are exempt.

Before leaving Mecca, every pilgrim must perform a farewell tawaf called Tawaf al-Wada' (also Tawaf al-Sadr). This passage establishes its legal status as wajib — a binding act that must be performed unless one is from Mecca or has taken up residence there. Failure to perform it without valid reason requires a sacrifice (dam). This ruling is unique to the Hanafi school; the Maliki school considers it sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text says: 'وهذا طواف الوداع ... وهذا واجب'. Recognize that Tawaf al-Wada' is named and explicitly classified as wajib.
It is also called Tawaf al-Sadr.
2
Note the exception
The exception: 'إلا على أهل مكة ومن أقام بها' — except for the people of Mecca and those residing there. Understand that this exemption is based on continuous residence.
The 'people of Mecca' includes those living there permanently; 'those residing there' includes temporary residents who intend to stay.
3
Understand the consequence of omission
Since it is wajib, intentional omission without a valid excuse requires a sacrifice (dam). The text does not explicitly state the penalty, but Hanafi fiqh sources confirm this.
If omitted unintentionally, no penalty is due according to some Hanafi authorities.
4
Perform the associated sunnah
After the tawaf, the pilgrim prays two rakats. The text says: 'ويصلي بعده ركعتين'. This is a sunnah act, not part of the wajib.
The two rakats are independent of the wujub of the tawaf itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره المبيت بغير منى ليالي الرمي، ثم إذا رحل إلى مكة نزل بالمحصب ساعة، ثم يدخل مكة، ويطوف بالبيت سبعة أشواط بلا رمل وسعي إن قدّمهما، وهذا طواف الوداع، ويسمى أيضا طواف الصدر، وهذا واجب إلا على أهل مكة ومن أقام بها، ويصلي بعده ركعتين
And it is makruh to stay overnight in other than Mina during the nights of ramy. Then when he departs to Mecca, he stops at al-Muhassab for an hour, then enters Mecca and performs tawaf of the House seven circuits without rapid walking (ramal) or running (sa'y) if he had already done them. This is Tawaf al-Wada', also called Tawaf al-Sadr. This is wajib except for the people of Mecca and those residing there. He then prays two rakats after it.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage covers two issues: (1) Staying overnight during the nights of ramy (11th, 12th, and if needed 13th) should be done in Mina. It is makruh tanzihi to stay elsewhere without excuse, but no penalty. (2) After finishing ramy and departing Mina, the pilgrim stops at al-Muhassab (a place between Mecca and Mina) for some time, then enters Mecca to perform Tawaf al-Wada' (farewell tawaf). This tawaf is performed without ramal (rapid walking) or sa'y if those were already done in Tawaf al-Qudum. It is also called Tawaf al-Sadr. This tawaf is wajib for every pilgrim except those who are from Mecca or have taken up residence there. After the tawaf, two rakats are prayed.

Key Terms
طواف الوداع (tawaf al-wada'): The farewell tawaf performed before leaving Mecca, wajib on non-Meccans.
المحصب (al-muhassab): A place between Mecca and Mina covered with small pebbles; the Prophet (SAW) stopped there.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is exempt from Tawaf al-Wada'?
A) All women.
B) People of Mecca and those residing there.
C) Pilgrims who have already performed Tawaf al-Ifadah.
D) Only the elderly.

Key Takeaway
Tawaf al-Wada' is wajib on all pilgrims except Meccans and residents; omission requires a sacrifice but does not invalidate Hajj.

Sunnah Acts for Drinking Zamzam Water
Sunnah actions for drinking Zamzam water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the sunnah actions when drinking Zamzam water and explain the intention-based principle associated with it.

Core Idea
Drink Zamzam water with intention and sunnah for maximum blessing.

This lesson covers the recommended etiquettes (adab) of drinking Zamzam water as outlined in Nur al-Idah. These actions enhance the spiritual benefit and follow the practice of the Companions, especially Abdullah ibn Abbas (RA). Understanding these sunnah practices helps you perform this act of worship with mindfulness and devotion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah acts
Read the passage and list the specific actions recommended when drinking Zamzam water, such as drawing water yourself, facing the Kaaba, drinking to satiety, etc.
Focus on the verbs describing what one should do.
2
Understand the intention
Reflect on the hadith 'Zamzam water is for whatever it is drunk for.' This means the benefit depends on one's sincere intention.
Intention can be for worldly or spiritual needs.
3
Recognize the du'a
Learn the du'a recited by Ibn Abbas (RA) asking for beneficial knowledge, abundant provision, and cure from every disease.
This du'a is recommended but not obligatory.
4
Apply the sunnah
Consider how to implement these actions in a modern context, such as at the Zamzam dispensing stations in the Grand Mosque.
Some actions may be adapted if not possible; they remain sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يأتي زمزم فيشرب من مائها ويستخرج الماء منها بنفسه إن قدر، ويستقبل البيت، ويتضلع منه، ويتنفس فيه مرارا، ويرفع بصره كل مرة ينظر إلى البيت، ويصب على جسده إن تيسر، وإلا يمسح به وجهه ورأسه وينوي بشربه ما شاء، وكان عبد الله بن عباس ما إذا شرب يقول: "اللهم إني أسألك علما نافعا، ورزقا واسعا، وشفاء من كل داء"، وقال : "ماء زمزم لما شرب له"
Then he comes to Zamzam and drinks from its water, and he draws the water out himself if he can. He faces the House, drinks to satiety, breathes in it several times, and lifts his sight each time looking at the House. He pours it on his body if possible, otherwise wipes his face and head with it. He intends by his drinking whatever he wishes. Abdullah ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) used to say when drinking: 'O Allah, I ask You for beneficial knowledge, abundant provision, and cure from every disease.' And he said: 'The water of Zamzam is for whatever it is drunk for.'
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage outlines the sunnah (recommended) actions when drinking Zamzam water. These include: drawing water oneself if possible, facing the Kaaba, drinking to satiety (being filled), breathing out into the container three times, looking at the Kaaba while drinking, and pouring some water on the body (or wiping face and head if pouring is not feasible). One should make intention for whatever good one desires. The du'a of Ibn Abbas is mentioned: asking for beneficial knowledge, abundant provision, and cure from every disease. The famous hadith 'The water of Zamzam is for whatever it is drunk for' is also cited, indicating that one should have a sincere intention when drinking.

Key Terms
زمزم (zamzam): The well in Mecca, whose water is blessed and has spiritual benefits.
يتضلع (yatadallu'): To drink to satiety, being filled with water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should one recite when drinking Zamzam water according to Ibn Abbas (RA)?
A) Bismillah
B) Du'a for knowledge, provision, and cure
C) Surah al-Fatiha
D) Takbir

Key Takeaway
Drinking Zamzam water is an act of worship enhanced by following the sunnah and making a sincere intention.

Ruling on Spending the Nights of Stoning in Mina
States the ruling on staying overnight in Mina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on staying outside Mina during the nights of tashriq and identify whether any compensation is required.

Core Idea
Leaving Mina at night is makruh, but no blood sacrifice needed — ease for the pilgrim.

During hajj, the nights of the 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul Hijjah are spent in Mina. The Hanafi school considers it disliked to stay elsewhere without excuse, but does not impose any penalty. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its basis in ease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
Recognize that the act under discussion is staying overnight (al-mabit) in Mina specifically during the nights of stoning (layali al-ramy).
These nights are after the Day of Sacrifice.
2
Note the ruling
The text states it is makruh (disliked) to stay elsewhere. In Hanafi terminology, this is makruh tanzihi, not tahrimi.
Makruh tanzihi means it is better to avoid but not sinful.
3
Understand the consequence
If one stays elsewhere without an excuse (ghayr 'udhr), nothing is required (la yalzamuhu shay'). No dam (sacrifice) or any compensation.
This is the clear Hanafi position.
4
Contrast with other schools
Some schools consider staying overnight in Mina as wajib and require dam for omission. The Hanafi school differs, viewing it as a sunnah act.
This ikhtilaf is recognized.
5
Recognize the underlying principle
The Hanafi school does not require compensation for omitting acts that are not wajib. Since mabit is not a pillar or wajib act in hajj, no penalty applies.
This reflects the principle of ease (yusr) in hajj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره المبيت بغير منى ليالي الرمي، ثم إذا رحل إلى مكة نزل بالمحصب ساعة، ولو بات في غيره من غير عذر لا يلزمه شيء عندنا. بالمحصب: اسم موضع، الأبطح، وهو موضع ذو حصى بين مكة ومنى، نزل به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم
And it is disliked to stay overnight elsewhere other than Mina on the nights of stoning. Then, when he departs to Makkah, he should stop at al-Muhassab for an hour. And if he stays overnight elsewhere without an excuse, nothing is required upon him according to us. Al-Muhassab is the name of a place, al-Abtah, a place with pebbles between Makkah and Mina; the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) stopped there.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings related to the days of tashriq and departure from Mina. First, it is makruh tanzihi (disliked) to spend the nights of the 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul Hijjah anywhere other than Mina. However, if one does so without a valid excuse, no dam (sacrifice) or compensation is required according to the Hanafi school. This is based on ease; the ruling is one of dislike, not prohibition. Second, when the pilgrim leaves Mina to return to Makkah, it is sunnah (recommended) to stop briefly at al-Muhassab (also called al-Abtah), a valley between Makkah and Mina where the Prophet ﷺ halted. The stop is brief, not an overnight stay. The word 'al-Muhassab' refers to the pebbles there.

Key Terms
المبيت (al-mabit): Staying overnight during the nights of tashriq in Mina.
المحصب (al-muhassab): A place with pebbles between Makkah and Mina where the Prophet ﷺ stopped after departing Mina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required of a pilgrim who stays outside Mina during the nights of stoning without a valid excuse?
A) He must sacrifice a sheep (dam).
B) Nothing is required; it is only disliked.
C) His pilgrimage is invalid.
D) He must give charity (sadaqah).

Key Takeaway
Staying outside Mina without excuse is makruh but incurs no compensation in the Hanafi school.

The Sunnah of Stopping at al-Muhassab
Describes the recommended stop at al-Muhassab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the recommended act of stopping at al-Muhassab after departing Mina and explain why it is sunnah.

Core Idea
A brief pause at al-Muhassab revives a Prophetic practice — no sin if missed, but blessed if done.

After completing the stoning and departing Mina, the Prophet ﷺ would stop for a while at a place called al-Muhassab. The Hanafi school recommends this as a sunnah act, not a requirement. In this lesson, we explore its location, ruling, and significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended act
The act is 'nazil bi al-Muhassab' — stopping at al-Muhassab for a short time (saa'atan) after departing Mina on the way to Makkah.
It is not an overnight stay; just a brief halt.
2
Note the ruling
The text uses 'nazala' (he stopped) referring to the Prophet's action, indicating it is a sunnah (recommended). The Hanafi school considers it mustahabb.
Sunnah here means a practice of the Prophet, not obligatory.
3
Understand the location
Al-Muhassab (also called al-Abtah) is a valley with small pebbles between Makkah and Mina. The name comes from 'hasba' (pebbles).
The text provides a brief definition.
4
Recognize the consequence of omission
If one does not stop, no compensation or penalty is incurred. The pilgrimage remains valid.
This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that sunnah acts do not require compensation.
5
Appreciate the Prophetic example
The Prophet ﷺ stopped there, so following his example brings reward. The stop is brief, not a lengthy stay.
The text explicitly mentions the Prophet's action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره المبيت بغير منى ليالي الرمي، ثم إذا رحل إلى مكة نزل بالمحصب ساعة، ولو بات في غيره من غير عذر لا يلزمه شيء عندنا. بالمحصب: اسم موضع، الأبطح، وهو موضع ذو حصى بين مكة ومنى، نزل به رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم
And it is disliked to stay overnight elsewhere other than Mina on the nights of stoning. Then, when he departs to Makkah, he should stop at al-Muhassab for an hour. And if he stays overnight elsewhere without an excuse, nothing is required upon him according to us. Al-Muhassab is the name of a place, al-Abtah, a place with pebbles between Makkah and Mina; the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) stopped there.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings related to the days of tashriq and departure from Mina. First, it is makruh tanzihi (disliked) to spend the nights of the 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul Hijjah anywhere other than Mina. However, if one does so without a valid excuse, no dam (sacrifice) or compensation is required according to the Hanafi school. This is based on ease; the ruling is one of dislike, not prohibition. Second, when the pilgrim leaves Mina to return to Makkah, it is sunnah (recommended) to stop briefly at al-Muhassab (also called al-Abtah), a valley between Makkah and Mina where the Prophet ﷺ halted. The stop is brief, not an overnight stay. The word 'al-Muhassab' refers to the pebbles there.

Key Terms
المحصب (al-muhassab): A place with pebbles between Makkah and Mina where the Prophet ﷺ stopped after departing Mina.
المبيت (al-mabit): Staying overnight during the nights of tashriq in Mina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of al-Muhassab according to the passage?
A) A mountain near Arafat.
B) A place with pebbles between Makkah and Mina.
C) A valley in Makkah.
D) The plain of Mina.

Key Takeaway
Stopping at al-Muhassab after departing Mina is a sunnah act based on the Prophet's example; no penalty for omitting it.

intermediate
6 min
The Ruling and Names of the Farewell Tawaf
Passage defining the farewell tawaf, its obligation, names, and a cross-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling of the farewell tawaf, list its five names, identify who is exempt, and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with the Maliki school.

Core Idea
The farewell tawaf is wajib except for Makkans – departing from the House obligates adieu.

The farewell tawaf (tawaf al-wada') is the final circumambulation a pilgrim performs before leaving Makkah. This lesson clarifies its legal status in the Hanafi school – it is wajib (obligatory), not merely recommended. We will also learn the five names by which this tawaf is known, the exceptions to its obligation, and an important inter-madhhab difference: Imam Malik considered it sunnah. Understanding this distinction affects whether a dam (sacrifice) is required if omitted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its Hanafi ruling
The passage begins by naming the act: 'this is the farewell tawaf... and this is wajib'. Confirm that the Hanafi school classifies it as wajib, not fard or sunnah.
Wajib in Hanafi fiqh means obligatory with zanni evidence – omission requires a dam but does not invalidate Hajj.
2
Note the exemption and its rationale
The text exempts 'ahl Makkah' (people of Makkah) and those who have taken up residence there. The rationale: tawaf al-wada' is wajib because of 'parting from the House' – they do not part from it.
The exemption extends to those within the miqat, as they can enter Makkah without ihram, like residents.
3
List the five names and their meanings
The passage enumerates: tawaf al-sadr (departure tawaf), tawaf al-wada' (farewell), tawaf al-ifadah (pour-forth, here synonymous with farewell), tawaf akhir al-ahd (last tawaf), and tawaf al-wajib (obligatory tawaf). Understand the etymons.
tawaf al-ifadah is more commonly the tawaf of the day of sacrifice; this passage uses it as a synonym for farewell – a nuance.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam Malik held that tawaf al-wada' is sunnah (recommended). Contrast the proofs: the Hanafi school relies on the practice of the Prophet ﷺ and the principle of bidding farewell, while the Maliki school views it as less binding.
The practical consequence: Hanafi – omission requires dam; Maliki – no penalty, but it is recommended to perform it.
5
Apply the ruling to a real scenario
Consider a pilgrim from outside Makkah who completes Hajj and plans to return. He must perform tawaf al-wada' before leaving. If he misses it due to hurry, he must slaughter a sheep as dam.
The exception: a Makkan pilgrim or one residing within the miqat need not perform it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا طواف الوداع، ويسمى أيضا طواف الصدر، وهذا واجب إلا على أهل مكة ومن أقام بها، ويصلي بعده ركعتين، ثم يأتي زمزم. وقال مالك: هو سنة. وله خمسة أسام: طواف الصدر؛ لأنه يصدر عنه أي يرجع، والصدر: الرجوع، وطواف الوداع؛ لأنه يودع البيت به، وطواف الإفاضة؛ لأنه لأجله يفيض إلى البيت من منى، وطواف آخر عهد بالبيت؛ لأنه لا طواف بعده، وطواف الواجب. إلا على إلخ لأنه يجب بمفارقة البيت وتوديعه وهم لا يفارقونه ولا يصدرون عنه، وكذا من كان في حكم أهل مكة من أهل المواقيت ومن دونها إلى مكة؛ لأنهم في حكم أهل مكة بدليل جواز دخولهم مكة بغير إحرام.
And this is the farewell tawaf, also called tawaf al-sadr, and this is obligatory except for the people of Makkah and those who have taken up residence there. And he prays two rak'ahs after it, then comes to Zamzam. And Malik said: It is sunnah. And it has five names: tawaf al-sadr because he departs from it, i.e., returns; al-sadr means return; tawaf al-wada' because he bids farewell to the House with it; tawaf al-ifadah because for its sake he pours forth from Mina to the House; tawaf akhir al-ahd bi al-bayt because there is no tawaf after it; and tawaf al-wajib. 'Except for' etc. because it becomes obligatory by parting from the House and bidding it farewell, and they (the Makkans) do not part from it nor depart from it. Likewise, those who are in the ruling of the people of Makkah from the people of the miqat and those below it up to Makkah; because they are in the ruling of the people of Makkah, evidenced by the permissibility of entering Makkah without ihram.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines the farewell tawaf (tawaf al-wada') and clarifies its ruling. The Hanafi school holds that this tawaf is wajib (obligatory) for every pilgrim who intends to leave Makkah, except for the people of Makkah and those residing there, as they do not actually part from the city. The text mentions that Imam Malik considered it sunnah (recommended), highlighting an inter-school difference. The passage then lists the five names for this tawaf: (1) tawaf al-sadr (departure tawaf), (2) tawaf al-wada' (farewell tawaf), (3) tawaf al-ifadah (which is also the name for the tawaf of the day of sacrifice, but here it is used synonymously for the farewell tawaf — this is a nuance), (4) tawaf akhir al-ahd (last tawaf), and (5) tawaf al-wajib (obligatory tawaf). The commentary explains that the exemption applies not only to Makkans but also to those living within the miqat boundaries, because they can enter Makkah without ihram, implying they are like residents.

Key Terms
طواف الوداع (tawaf al-wada'): The farewell circumambulation performed before leaving Makkah after Hajj.
طواف الصدر (tawaf al-sadr): Another name for the farewell tawaf, derived from 'sadr' meaning return/departure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Women and children.
D) Only the poor and destitute.

Key Takeaway
The farewell tawaf is wajib for every pilgrim departing Makkah, except residents and miqat dwellers, and its omission requires a dam.

Recommended Acts After Tawaf: Zamzam and Multazam
Describes the recommended acts of drinking Zamzam and supplicating at the multazam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the recommended acts of drinking Zamzam and supplicating at the multazam after the farewell tawaf, including the proper etiquettes and intentions.

Core Idea
Zamzam water is for whatever you drink it for; the multazam is a place where supplications are answered.

After completing the farewell tawaf, the pilgrim is encouraged to perform two virtuous acts: drinking from the blessed well of Zamzam and supplicating at the multazam, the area between the Black Stone and the Kaaba door. This lesson details the recommended mannerisms and intentions for these acts, as derived from the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text describes two recommended acts: drinking Zamzam and going to the multazam. Both are mustahabb (recommended) after the farewell tawaf.
Mustahabb means performing it is rewarded, but omitting it is not sinful.
2
List the etiquettes for drinking Zamzam
From the text: face the Kaaba, drink to satiety with deep breaths, raise your sight to the House, pour some on your body if possible, and make a sincere intention for whatever need you have.
The hadith 'Zamzam water is for what it is drunk for' implies intention matters.
3
Explain the recommended actions at the multazam
After drinking, go to the door of the Kaaba, kiss the threshold (if possible), then move to the multazam. Press your chest and face against it, cling to the curtains, and supplicate humbly for worldly and religious needs.
A sample supplication is provided in the text.
4
Note the significance of the multazam
The multazam is one of the fifteen places in Makkah where supplications are answered, highlighting its high virtue.
5
Apply the recommended acts in practice
When performing Hajj or Umrah, after the farewell tawaf, intentionally perform these acts with the described etiquettes to gain maximum reward.
These acts are not obligatory but highly recommended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يأتي زمزم فيشرب من مائها ويستخرج الماء منها بنفسه إن قدر، ويستقبل البيت، ويتضلع منه، ويتنفس فيه مرارا، ويرفع بصره كل مرة ينظر إلى البيت، ويصب على جسده إن تيسر، وإلا يمسح به وجهه ورأسه وينوي بشربه ما شاء، وكان عبد الله بن عباس ما إذا شرب يقول: 'اللهم إني أسألك علما نافعا، ورزقا واسعا، وشفاء من كل داء'، وقال: 'ماء زمزم لما شرب له'. ويستحب بعد شربه أن يأتي باب الكعبة ويقبل العتبة ثم يأتي إلى الملتزم، وهو ما بين الحجر الأسود والباب، فيضع صدره ووجهه عليه، ويتشبث بأستار الكعبة ساعة يتضرع إلى الله تعالى بالدعاء بما أحب من أمور الدارين، ويقول: 'اللهم إن هذا بيتك الذي جعلته مباركا وهدى للعالمين، اللهم كما هديتني له فتقبل مني، ولا تجعل هذا آخر العهد من بيتك وارزقني العود إليه حتى ترضى عني برحمتك يا أرحم الراحمين'، والملتزم من الأماكن التي يستجاب فيها الدعاء بمكة المشرفة، وهي خمسة عشر
Then he comes to Zamzam and drinks from its water, and extracts the water from it himself if he can, facing the House, and drinks to satiety, and breathes in it several times, and raises his sight each time looking at the House, and pours it on his body if possible, otherwise he wipes his face and head with it, and intends by his drinking whatever he wishes. And Abdullah ibn Abbas used to say when he drank: 'O Allah, I ask You for beneficial knowledge, abundant provision, and cure from every disease.' And he said: 'The water of Zamzam is for what it is drunk for.' And it is recommended after drinking it that he comes to the door of the Kaaba and kisses the threshold, then comes to the multazam, which is between the Black Stone and the door, places his chest and face upon it, clings to the curtains of the Kaaba for a while, supplicating humbly to Allah Almighty for whatever he wishes of the affairs of this world and the Hereafter, and says: 'O Allah, this is Your House which You have made blessed and guidance for the worlds. O Allah, just as You guided me to it, accept it from me, and do not make this the last time with Your House, and grant me return to it until You are pleased with me, by Your mercy, O Most Merciful of the merciful.' And the multazam is among the places where supplication is answered in honorable Makkah, and they are fifteen.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
After performing the farewell tawaf, the pilgrim is recommended to drink from the water of Zamzam. The text details the etiquettes: facing the Kaaba, drinking to satiety (taking deep breaths), pouring some on the body, and making a specific intention for the drinking. The hadith 'Maa'u Zamzama lima shuriba lah' (Zamzam water is for whatever it is drunk for) is cited, encouraging sincere intentions. Then, it is recommended to go to the door of the Kaaba, kiss the threshold (al-utbah), and proceed to the multazam — the area between the Black Stone and the Kaaba door — where one presses the chest and face, clings to the curtains, and supplicates. A sample supplication is given. The text notes that the multazam is one of fifteen places in Makkah where supplications are answered, implying its high virtue.

Key Terms
زمزم (zamzam): The blessed well in Makkah near the Kaaba, from which pilgrims drink.
الملتزم (al-multazam): The area between the Black Stone and the door of the Kaaba where one presses the body and supplicates.
يستحب (yustahabb): It is recommended; a legal ruling indicating reward for doing and no sin for leaving.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Walk around the Kaaba seven times.

Key Takeaway
After the farewell tawaf, pilgrims should drink Zamzam with intention and etiquettes, then supplicate at the multazam, a place where du'as are answered.

Places of Accepted Supplication in Mecca: The Multazam and Fifteen Other Locations
Describes the Multazam and enumerates fifteen places where supplication is accepted in Mecca.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the fifteen places in Mecca where supplication is accepted and describe the recommended practice at al-Multazam.

Core Idea
There are fifteen named locations in Mecca where supplication is especially answered; al-Multazam is one of them.

This lesson covers the recommended acts at al-Multazam after drinking Zamzam, and the fifteen places in Mecca where supplication is accepted. The text provides a list and a poem enumerating these places, emphasizing their spiritual significance. As a Hanafi fiqh ruling_statement, the lesson clarifies that these acts are mustahabb (recommended), not obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended act
After drinking Zamzam, the pilgrim goes to al-Multazam and supplicates. This is a mustahabb act, not obligatory.
The text uses 'yustahabb' (it is recommended).
2
Recognize the definition of al-Multazam
Al-Multazam is the area between the Black Stone and the door of the Kaaba. The pilgrim places his chest and face on it, clings to the curtains, and supplicates.
This is a specific location with known boundaries.
3
Memorize the fifteen places
The places include: Tawaf area, Multazam, inside the Kaaba at Asr, under the water-spout at Sahar, at Zamzam well, behind Maqam, on Safa and Marwa, in Sa'i, in Arafat, in Mina, at Jamarat, and at the sight of the Kaaba. The poem enumerates fifteen locations.
The list comes from a poem by al-Naqqash and a letter from Hasan al-Basri.
4
Understand the source
The list is transmitted by Kamal ibn al-Humam from Hasan al-Basri's letter. The poem by al-Naqqash also lists them.
Multiple authorities confirm these places.
5
Apply the practice
During Hajj or Umrah, the pilgrim should make dua at these places, especially at al-Multazam with the prescribed supplication.
There is no sin if one omits these; they are recommended.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on Hajj detailing the recommended acts after drinking Zamzam water. The author describes the etiquette at al-Multazam (the space between the Black Stone and the Kaaba door), where the pilgrim should place his chest and face, cling to the curtains, and supplicate with specific words. This is a mustahabb (recommended) act. The text then explains that al-Multazam is one of fifteen locations in Mecca where supplication is especially answered. The fifteen places are enumerated through a poem by Muhammad al-Naqqash and a later clarification by al-Kamal ibn al-Humam, citing a letter from Hasan al-Basri. The author also includes a personal anecdote about drinking Zamzam and a note about the fifteen places. The overall tone is instructional, emphasizing the spiritual value of these locations.

Key Terms
الملتزم (al-multazam): The area between the Black Stone and the door of the Kaaba where it is recommended to place one's chest and face and supplicate.
المطاف (al-matāf): The area of Tawaf around the Kaaba.

Entering the Kaaba: Recommendation and Refuting a False Belief
Ruling statement with critical refinement of a false belief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on entering the Kaaba, identify the correct prayer spot and etiquette, and distinguish the baseless green tile claim.

Core Idea
Entering the Kaaba is recommended (mustahabb) when it doesn't cause harm; the green tile is not the Prophet's prayer spot but a baseless innovation.

The Kaaba is the holiest site in Islam, and entering it is a blessed act. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling: it is recommended (mustahabb) as long as it does not inconvenience others. The passage also warns against a common misconception—the green tile between two pillars is not the Prophet's prayer place, and calling it "al-ʿurwa al-wuthqā" is an unfounded innovation. Learn the correct etiquette and avoid bidʿah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed ruling on entering the Kaaba
Read the opening statement: 'ويستحب دخول البيت الشريف المبارك إن لم يؤذ أحدا.' The word يستحب indicates recommendation (istihbāb). Confirm the condition: 'if it does not harm anyone.'
The condition prevents harm to others; if harm occurs, the act loses its recommendation.
2
Locate the Prophet's prayer spot and the correct posture
The text describes the Prophet's prayer spot: with the door behind him, facing a wall about three cubits away. This is where one should pray. Then describe the recommended actions: placing cheek on the wall, seeking forgiveness, praising, and visiting corners with dhikr.
The green tile is explicitly denied as the prayer spot.
3
Identify the false claim and its refutation
The text states: 'وليست البلاطة الخضراء التي بين العمودين مصلى النبي... مبتدأ من أنه العروة الوثقى... بدعة باطلة لا أصل لها.' Recognize this as a critical refinement: the common belief is baseless and an innovation.
The innovation (bidʿah) is false attribution of a specific spot.
4
Examine the dalīl for entering the Kaaba
The text quotes a hadith: 'من دخل البيت دخل في حسنة، وخرج من سيئة مغفورا له.' This is an ahad hadith (zannī al-thubūt) and supports istihbāb through promise of reward.
Hanafi usul: a promise of reward indicates recommendation, not obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حاشية الطحطاوي: ٧٣٧]
وذكرنا إلخ وعن عطاء أنه عليها كان يقول إذا لقي البيت: أعوذ برب البيت من الكفر والفقر، ومن ضيق الصدر وعذاب القبر، ويرفع يديه ومن أهم الأدعية طلب الجنة بلا حساب فإن الدعاء مستجاب عند رؤية البيت. [فتح القدير: ٣٥٢/٢]
ويستحب إلخ: واعلم أن دخول البيت مستحب إذا لم يؤذ أحدا، ثبت دخوله إياه، وأنه كبر في نواحيه، وعن ابن عباس عنه عليها من دخل البيت دخل في حسنة، وخرج من سيئة مغفورا له رواه البيهقي وغيره، وقالت "عجبا للمرء المسلم إذا دخل الكعبة كيف يرفع بصره قبل السقف يدع ذلك إجلالا لله تعالى وإعظاما، دخل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ما خلف بصره موضع سجوده حتى خرج منهما. [فتح القدير بحذف: ٣٩١/٢]
عائشة
It is recommended to enter the noble, blessed House if it does not harm anyone. He should aim for the prayer place of the Prophet inside it, which is facing his direction, and he should place the door behind his back so that between him and the wall facing him there is about three cubits, then pray. When he prays towards the wall, he puts his cheek on it, seeks forgiveness and praises Allah. Then he goes to the corners, praising, declaring His oneness, glorifying and magnifying, and asks Allah for whatever he wishes. He should maintain proper conduct outwardly and inwardly as much as possible. The green tile between the two pillars is not the prayer place of the Prophet. What the common people say, that it is al-urwa al-wuthqa and it is a high spot on the wall of the Kaaba, is a baseless innovation with no foundation. ... And we have mentioned etc. And from Ata that he used to say when meeting the House: 'I seek refuge with the Lord of the House from disbelief and poverty, and from distress of the chest and punishment of the grave,' and he would raise his hands. Among the most important supplications is to ask for Paradise without reckoning, for the supplication is answered upon seeing the House. ... It is recommended to enter the House if it does not harm anyone. It is established that he entered it and that he said the takbir in its corners. Ibn Abbas narrated from him that whoever enters the House enters into a good deed and leaves a sin forgiven. It was reported by Bayhaqi and others. Aisha said: 'It is amazing for a Muslim man when he enters the Kaaba, how he should raise his eyes to the ceiling? He should avoid that out of reverence and awe for Allah. The Messenger of Allah entered and did not let his gaze go beyond his place of prostration until he came out of it.'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage provides two main rulings: the recommendation to enter the Kaaba, and the refutation of a false belief regarding the green tile. The first part states that entering the Kaaba is mustahabb (recommended) provided it does not cause harm or inconvenience to others. The pilgrim should locate the Prophet's prayer spot, which is identified by standing with the door behind him and the wall in front about three cubits away. He should pray, place his cheek on the wall, seek forgiveness, and then visit the corners making dhikr. The second part is a critical refinement of a common error: the green tile (بلاطة خضراء) between two pillars is not the Prophet's prayer spot, and the claim that it is 'al-urwa al-wuthqa' (the firm handhold) is a baseless innovation. The author quotes al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah to label this as a bid'ah (innovation). The passage then cites additional narrations about entering the Kaaba, including a hadith from Aisha that the Prophet kept his gaze lowered during his entry. The overall message is to perform this act with proper etiquette and avoid innovated practices.

Key Terms
البلاطة الخضراء (al-balāṭa al-khaḍrā'): The green tile between two pillars inside the Kaaba which some mistakenly believe is the Prophet's prayer spot.
العروة الوثقى (al-ʿurwat al-wuthqā): A term the common people use for the green tile, claiming it is a firm handhold; the author refutes this as baseless.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on entering the Kaaba based on the passage?
A) It is obligatory (wājib).
B) It is recommended (mustahabb) provided it does not harm anyone.
C) It is permissible (mubāḥ) without any condition.
D) It is disliked (makrūh).

Key Takeaway
Entering the Kaaba is a recommended act with conditions; avoid the baseless innovation of the green tile.

Etiquette of Entering the Kaaba and Refuting Baseless Practices
Recommends acts inside Kaaba and refutes bid'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended acts when entering the Kaaba and identify which common practices are bid'ah according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Entering the Kaaba is sunnah; but the green slab and nail have no special status — their veneration is bid'ah.

Entering the Kaaba is a great blessing, but it must be done with proper adab. This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the sunnah acts inside the House and, more importantly, corrects two widespread innovations: the false reverence for the green slab and the nail called the 'navel of the world.' Knowing what is sunnah and what is bid'ah protects your worship and keeps it in line with the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended places for supplication
List the ten places mentioned where du'a is mustahabb: during tawaf, at Multazam, under the mizab, inside the House, at Zamzam, behind Maqam Ibrahim, on Safa and Marwah, during sa'i, at Arafat, at Mina, and at the Jamarat.
These are based on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the early Muslims.
2
Outline the etiquette of entering the Kaaba
Describe the recommended actions: aim for the Prophet's prayer spot (facing the direction of his face, with the door behind, about three cubits from the front wall), pray, place cheek on the wall, seek forgiveness, then go to corners praising Allah.
This adab is supported by hadith and Hanafi fiqh.
3
Refute the false belief about the green slab
State clearly: the green slab between the two pillars is NOT the Prophet's prayer place. The common claim that it is 'al-urwat al-wuthqa' (the firmest handhold) is a baseless innovation (bid'ah).
Know this to avoid falling into common error when visiting the Kaaba.
4
Refute the false belief about the nail
The nail in the center of the Kaaba, called 'navel of the world', has no religious significance. Uncovering private parts to touch it is foolish and ignorant, as stated by al-Kamal ibn al-Humam.
This is a practical reminder to maintain haya' and avoid senseless acts.
5
Describe the farewell tawaf
When leaving, perform the farewell circumambulation (tawaf al-wada'), then walk backwards facing the Kaaba, weeping or feigning weeping, until leaving the mosque. Exit through the Banu Shaybah gate.
This shows deep love and respect for the House of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيفية تركيب أفعال الحج موضعا نقلها الكمال بن الهمام عن رسالة الحسن البصري حله بقوله: في الطواف، وعند الملتزم، وتحت الميزاب وفي البيت وعند زمزم وخلف المقام، وعلى الصفا، وعلى المروة، وفي السعي وفي عرفات وفي من، وعند الجمرات. والجمرات ترمى في أربعة أيام يوم النحر، وثلاثة بعده كما تقدم، وذكرنا استجابته أيضا عند رؤية البيت المكرم، ويستحب دخول البيت الشريف المبارك إن لم يؤذ أحدا، وينبغي أن يقصد مصلى النبي فيه، وهو قبل وجهه، وقد جعل الباب قبل ظهره، حتى يكون بينه وبين الجدار الذي قبل وجهه قرب ثلاثة أذرع، ثم يصلي، فإذا صلى إلى الجدار يضع خده عليه، ويستغفر الله ويحمده، ثم يأتي الأركان، فيحمد ويهلل ويسبح ويكبر، ويسأل الله تعالى ما شاء، ويلزم الأدب ما استطاع بظاهره وباطنه، وليست البلاطة الخضراء التي بين العمودين مصلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وما تقوله العامة أنه العروة الوثقى وهو موضع عال في جدار البيت بدعة باطلة لا أصل لها، والمسمار الذي في وسط البيت يسمّونه سرّة الدنيا، يكشف أحدهم عورته وسرته ويضعها عليه فعل من لا عقل له فضلا عن علم كما قاله الكمال، وإذا أراد العود إلى أهله ينبغي أن ينصرف بعد طوافه للوداع، وهو يمشي إلى ورائه ووجهه إلى البيت باكيا أو متباكيا متحسّرا على فراق البيت حتى يخرج من المسجد، ويخرج من مكة من باب بني شيبة من الثنية السفلى.
The manner of performing the actions of Hajj: Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam transmitted it from the treatise of al-Hasan al-Basri, solving it with his statement: During the circumambulation, at the Multazam, under the water spout, inside the House, at Zamzam, behind the Maqam, on Safa, on Marwah, during the sa'i, at Arafat, at Mina, at the Jamarat. The Jamarat are stoned on four days: the Day of Sacrifice and three days after it as mentioned earlier. We also mentioned its recommendation at the sight of the honored Kaaba. It is recommended to enter the noble blessed House if one does not harm anyone. He should aim for the Prophet's prayer place in it, which is facing the direction of his face, and he should place the door behind his back so that there is about three cubits between him and the wall that is in front of his face, then pray. When he prays towards the wall, he places his cheek on it, seeks forgiveness from Allah and praises Him, then goes to the corners, praising, declaring His oneness, glorifying, and magnifying, and asks Allah Almighty for whatever he wishes, and maintains etiquette as much as possible outwardly and inwardly. The green slab that is between the two pillars is NOT the Prophet's prayer place, and what the common people say that it is al-urwat al-wuthqa (the firmest handhold) and that it is a high place on the wall of the House is an innovated falsehood with no basis. The nail that is in the center of the House, which they call the navel of the world, and someone uncovers his private parts and navel and places them on it, is an act of one who has no intellect, let alone knowledge, as al-Kamal said. When he intends to return to his family, he should depart after performing the farewell circumambulation, walking backwards, with his face towards the House, weeping or feigning weeping, regretful at leaving the House, until he exits the mosque, and he leaves Mecca from the Banu Shaybah gate at the lower hill.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah with its hashiya discusses the recommended acts related to the Kaaba. It first lists places where du'a is recommended: during tawaf, at Multazam, under the mizab, etc. Then it details the etiquette of entering the Kaaba: aiming for the Prophet's prayer spot, placing the door behind, leaving three cubits, praying, placing cheek on the wall, seeking forgiveness. A crucial point: the green slab between the pillars is NOT the Prophet's prayer place; the common belief that it is 'al-urwat al-wuthqa' is an innovation (bid'ah) with no basis. Similarly, the nail in the center, falsely called 'navel of the world', has no significance; exposing one's private parts to it is foolish and ignorant. Finally, the pilgrim should perform the farewell tawaf and leave walking backwards, weeping, from the Banu Shaybah gate. All these rulings are mustahabb (recommended) according to the Hanafi school, as derived from the sunna and the practice of the righteous predecessors.

Key Terms
البلاطة الخضراء (al-balāṭa al-khaḍrāʾ): The green slab between the two pillars inside the Kaaba, which some falsely claim to be the Prophet's prayer place and a sacred spot.
المسمار (al-mismār): A nail in the center of the Kaaba floor, called 'navel of the world' by common people, with no religious significance.
العروة الوثقى (al-ʿurwa al-wuthqā): A term meaning 'the firmest handhold', falsely applied by common people to the green slab or a spot on the wall; not based on any authentic source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is the Prophet's prayer place, so it is recommended to pray there.
B) It is a place of accepted supplication (al-urwat al-wuthqa).
C) The common belief about it is an innovation with no basis.
D) It is sunnah to touch it and place one's cheek on it.

Key Takeaway
Entering the Kaaba is sunnah, but the green slab and nail have no special status—follow the guidance of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and avoid innovations.

Women's Rites in Hajj: Modifications for Modesty
Lists specific differences in Hajj rites for women due to modesty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven specific differences in Hajj rites for women compared to men according to the Hanafi school, and explain the rationale for each.

Core Idea
A woman's Hajj is like a man's except where modesty requires modification: covering, quiet, no rushing, and no shaving.

In this passage from Nur al-Idah, the author enumerates the specific ways a woman's Hajj differs from a man's. While the essential acts (arkan) are identical, certain sunan and wajibat are waived or replaced for women to preserve modesty. This lesson will guide you through each difference and its basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The author begins by stating that a woman is like a man in all actions of Hajj. This establishes the default: the same obligations, pillars, and rituals apply. Then the exceptions are listed.
Note the Hanafi approach: specify the general rule first, then carve out exceptions.
2
Classify each difference
For each exception, determine whether it is a wajib (obligatory modification), a sunnah (recommended avoidance), or a prohibition (haram). For example, covering the head is wajib for women always, while omitting ramal is sunnah.
The text does not explicitly classify the hukm of each, but the context and other Hanafi texts clarify.
3
Trace the rationale
Many differences stem from the principle of modesty (satr) and avoiding fitnah. For example, not raising the voice in talbiyah prevents attracting attention. Not crowding the Black Stone avoids mixing with men.
Understanding the rationale helps in applying analogous rulings.
4
Apply the taqsir ruling
The text explicitly says women do not shave but shorten. This is a wajib substitution. The hadith analogy: shaving for women is like a beard for men—a form of mutilation (muthlah) that is forbidden.
This is a unique Hanafi emphasis; other schools may allow shaving.
5
Appreciate the final comparison
The passage ends by ranking Hajj types (mufrid, tamattu', qiran). This contextualizes that these modifications apply regardless of the type of Hajj.
The ruling on virtue is based on the number of Umrahs performed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرأة في جميع أفعال الحج كالرجل غير أنها لا تكشف رأسها، وتسدل على وجهها شيئا تحته عيدان كالقبة تمنع مسه بالغطاء، ولا ترفع صوتها بالتلبية، بل تسمع نفسها، ولا ترمل بل تسمع نفسها ولا تهرول في السعي بين الميلين الأخضرين، بل تمشي على هينتها في جميع السعي بين الصفا والمروة، ولا تحلق وتقصر ولا تزاحم الرجال في استلام الحجر، وهذا تمام حج المفرد، وهو دون المتمتع في الفضل والقرآن أفضل من التمتع.
A woman in all actions of Hajj is like a man, except that she does not uncover her head, and she lets down over her face something that has sticks underneath like a dome to prevent it from touching the face, and she does not raise her voice with talbiyah, rather she makes herself hear it, and she does not perform ramal, and she does not hasten (harwala) in sa'i between the two green markers, rather she walks at her ease in all of sa'i between Safa and Marwah, and she does not shave but shortens (taqsir), and she does not crowd men in touching the black stone. This completes the Hajj of the individual (mufrid), which is less in virtue than tamattu', and qiran is better than tamattu'.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah states the principle: a woman's Hajj is identical to a man's except in six specific matters due to her need for modesty (satr). First, she does not uncover her head; men uncover theirs in ihram. Second, she drapes a cloth over her face with sticks to ensure it does not touch her skin (a precaution against the cloth touching the face while avoiding full face-covering, which is prohibited for men in ihram). Third, she does not raise her voice in talbiyah, only loud enough for herself to hear, to avoid attracting attention. Fourth, she does not do ramal (quick pace with short steps) in tawaf. Fifth, she does not do harwala (fast walking) in sa'i between Safa and Marwah; she walks normally. Sixth, she does not shave her head; she only shortens her hair, because shaving is considered a form of mutilation (muthlah) for women, analogous to shaving the beard for men. Seventh, she does not crowd men to touch the Black Stone. The passage ends by noting that the Hajj of mufrid (performing Hajj only) is lesser in virtue than tamattu' (performing Umrah then Hajj with a break), and qiran (combining Umrah and Hajj without a break) is best of all.

Key Terms
الترمل (al-ramal): A quick pace with short steps performed by men in the first three circuits of tawaf; women do not do it.
الهرولة (al-harwala): A moderate fast walk between the two green markers in sa'i (specifically for men); women do not do it.
التقصير (al-taqṣīr): Shortening the hair (for women) as a substitute for shaving after completing Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a difference between a woman's and a man's Hajj according to the passage?
A) She does not uncover her head.
B) She does not raise her voice in talbiyah.
C) She must shave her head at the end of Hajj.
D) She does not crowd men to touch the Black Stone.

Key Takeaway
A woman's Hajj is same as a man's except for seven modifications: covering, quiet, no ramal/harwala, no shaving, no crowding.

Departure Etiquette: Farewell Tawaf and Exit from Mecca
Ruling on departure sunna and exit etiquette

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sunna actions of the farewell circumambulation (tawaf al-wada') and the recommended manner of departing from Mecca.

Core Idea
The pilgrim departs with a weeping heart, walking backwards – the farewell tawaf is a sunna of intense spiritual significance.

After completing the rituals of Hajj, the pilgrim prepares to leave the sacred city. This lesson describes the emotional and physical conduct recommended by the Hanafi school: performing the farewell tawaf, then exiting the mosque while weeping and walking backwards, leaving from the historic Bab Bani Shayba. These actions embody the deep attachment to the House of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Recognize that the passage describes the farewell circumambulation (tawaf al-wada') and the subsequent exit from the mosque. This is a sunna of leaving the Haram.
2
Extract the sunna actions
List the recommended behaviours: walking backwards while facing the Kaaba, weeping or feigning weeping (tabaki), showing regret, and leaving specifically from Bab Bani Shayba (the lower Thaniyya).
Walking backwards symbolizes reluctance to turn one's back on the House.
3
Determine the ruling level
All these actions are sunna (mandub). They are not obligatory but highly meritorious. Omission does not invalidate the Hajj.
The text uses 'ينبغي' (should), indicating recommendation.
4
Note the criticism of superstition
The passage first criticizes a superstitious practice involving a nail in the Kaaba. This is extraneous to the ruling but shows the author's rejection of baseless acts.
This criticism is not part of the departure etiquette but serves as a caution.
5
Apply to practice
A pilgrim should plan to perform tawaf al-wada' before leaving Mecca, then exit in a humble, tearful manner, preferably from the gate of Bani Shayba.
If departing from another gate, the spirit of humility remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج
كيفية تركيب أفعال الحج والمسمار الذي في وسط البيت يسمّونه سرّة الدنيا، يكشف أحدهم عورته وسرته ويضعها عليه فعل من لا عقل له فضلا عن علم كما قاله الكمال، وإذا أراد العود إلى أهله أن ابن همام أي الحاج ينبغي ينصرف بعد طوافه للوداع، وهو يمشي إلى ورائه ووجهه إلى البيت باكيا أو متباكيا متحسّرا على فراق البيت حتى يخرج من المسجد، ويخرج من حال من المستتر في "يمشى" مكة من باب بني شيبة من الثنية السفلى.
والمرأة في جميع أفعال الحج كالرجل غير أنها لا تكشف رأسها، وتسدل على وجهها شيئا تحته عيدان كالقبة تمنع مسه بالغطاء، ولا ترفع صوتها بالتلبية، ولا ترمل بل تسمع نفسها، ولا تهرول في السعي بين الميلين الأخضرين، بل تمشي على هينتها في جميع السعي بين الصفا والمروة، ولا تحلق، وتقصر وتلبس المخيط، ولا تزاحم الرجال في استلام الحجر، وهذا تمام حج المفرد، وهو دون المتمتع في الفضل والقرآن أفضل من التمتع.
والمسمار : لا يوجد هذا اللفظ في فتح القدير.
متباكيا: اسم الفاعل من التباكي ومعناه: التكلف بالبكاء.
لا تكشف رأسها والمراد بكشف الوجه عدم مماسة شيء له فلذا يكره لها أن تلبس البرقع؛ لأن ذلك يماس وجهها، كذا في "المبسوط" [البحر الرائق: ٥٤١/٢]
وتسدل وفي فتح القدير: إنه يستحب، مشتق من سدل بمعنى إرخاء الثوب من نصر. [البحر الرائق: ٥٤١/٢]
ولا ترمل أشار إلى أنها لا تضطبع؛ لأنه سنة الرمل وهي لا ترمل [البحر الرائق بحذف: ٥٤١/٢]
ولا تهرول: المضارع المنفي من الهرولة وهو نوع السير بين العدو والمشي وتقصر أفاد أنها كالرجل فيه، خلافا لما قيل: إنه لا يتقدر في حقها بالربع، بخلاف الرجل. [البحر الرائق: ٥٤١/٢]
وتلبس المخيط: وكذا تلبس الخفين والقفازين. [البحر الرائق: ٥٤١/٢]
ولا تزاحم إلخ فإن قلت لم ترك الشيخ أحكاما مخصوصة بها؟ منها أنها لا تحج إلا بمحرم بخلاف الرجل، ومنها أنها تترك طواف الصدر بعذر الحيض كما صرّح الشارح الزيلعي في شرحه للكنز، قلت لأن مثل هذه الأحكام ليس مما نحن فيه؛ لأن الأول لا يختص بالحج، بل هو حكم كل سفر، وكذا الثاني؛ لأن الحيض غير ممكن من الرجل حتى تخالفه في أحكامه.
Book of Hajj. How to compose the actions of Hajj and the nail that is in the middle of the House, they call it the navel of the world; one of them uncovers his private part and his navel and places it upon it, an act of one who has no intellect let alone knowledge, as al-Kamal said. And when he wishes to return to his family, Ibn Humam (i.e. the pilgrim) should depart after his farewell circumambulation, walking backwards with his face towards the House, crying or feigning crying, regretful over leaving the House, until he leaves the mosque. And he leaves Mecca from the Bab Bani Shayba from the lower Thaniyya. And the woman in all actions of Hajj is like the man except that she does not uncover her head, and she lets down over her face something beneath which are sticks like a dome, preventing its touching the covering. She does not raise her voice in talbiya, and she does not do ramal, rather she makes herself hear; she does not run (harwala) in the sa'i between the two green markers, rather she walks at her ease in all the sa'i between Safa and Marwa. She does not shave her head, but she shortens, and she wears sewn garments, and she does not crowd men in kissing the Black Stone. This concludes the Hajj of ifrad, and it is inferior to tamattu' in virtue, and qiran is better than tamattu'. And the nail: this word is not found in Fath al-Qadir. Mutabakiyan: the active participle from tabaki, meaning pretending to cry. 'She does not uncover her head' and the meaning of uncovering the face is not touching anything to it, so it is disliked for her to wear the burqa' because that touches her face. So in al-Mabsut [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/541]. 'And she lets down' and in Fath al-Qadir: it is recommended, derived from sadala meaning letting the garment hang down from Nasr. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/541]. 'And she does not do ramal': he pointed out that she does not expose her shoulder because ramal is sunna and she does not do ramal. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq abridged 2/541]. 'And she does not run (harwala)': the verb negated from harwala which is a type of pace between running and walking. 'And she shortens' indicates she is like the man in it, contrary to what is said: it is not measured for her by a quarter, unlike the man. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/541]. 'And she wears sewn garments': likewise she wears khuffs and gloves. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/541]. 'And she does not crowd' etc. If you say: why did the author leave out rulings specific to her? Among them: she does not perform Hajj except with a mahram unlike the man, and she omits the farewell circumambulation due to the excuse of menstruation as al-Zayla'i stated in his commentary on al-Kanz. I say: because such rulings are not what we are discussing; because the first is not specific to Hajj, rather it is a ruling for every journey, and likewise the second, because menstruation is not possible for the man such that he differs from her in its rulings.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement covering two aspects of ifrad Hajj: departure etiquette and women's specific rulings. The author begins by criticizing a superstitious practice involving a nail in the Kaaba, then describes the sunna manner of departing: walking backwards, weeping, and leaving from Bab Bani Shayba. The main body addresses women's rulings: they cover their heads, let down a veil that does not touch the face (recommended), do not raise their voice in talbiya, do not perform ramal or harwala in tawaf and sa'i, do not shave but only shorten hair, wear sewn garments, and do not crowd men when kissing the Black Stone. The commentary explains the dislike of the burqa', the recommendation of draping the veil, and that women's pace in sa'i is not measured like men's. It also justifies why certain gender-specific rulings (mahram, menstruation) are omitted here, as they are general travel or innate differences. The passage ends by noting the relative virtue of ifrad, tamattu', and qiran.

Key Terms
الوداع (al-wada'): The farewell circumambulation (tawaf al-wada') performed before leaving Mecca after Hajj or Umrah.
التباكي (al-tabaki): Feigning weeping or making an effort to cry, recommended when departing the Haram.
باب بني شيبة (Bab Bani Shayba): A historical gate of the Masjid al-Haram, traditionally used by pilgrims when leaving after Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on walking backwards when leaving the Masjid al-Haram after the farewell tawaf?
A) It is wajib (obligatory).
B) It is sunna (recommended).
C) It is haram because it may cause harm.
D) It is makruh (disliked).

Key Takeaway
Departing the Haram with humility and regret is a sunna, not an obligation, but fulfilling it deepens the spiritual experience of Hajj.

Women's Specific Rulings in Hajj Actions
Ruling on women's specific modifications in Hajj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the specific differences between men and women in the actions of Hajj according to the Hanafi school and apply them in practice.

Core Idea
Woman in Hajj is like a man except where modesty and weakness require distinction: she covers, does not run, shortens hair, and avoids crowding.

This passage enumerates the specific concessions and rules for women in ihram and during Hajj rituals. While women share the same essential acts (tawaf, sa'i, standing at Arafat, etc.), they are exempted or required to modify certain actions to maintain modesty and avoid fitna. These include covering the head, lowering the voice in talbiya, not doing ramal or harwala, not shaving the head, wearing sewn garments, and not crowding men at the Black Stone. The rulings reflect the general principle that women are like men in worship except where textual evidence or public interest dictates otherwise.

Guided Path
1
State the overall rule
Affirm the baseline: 'المرأة في جميع أفعال الحج كالرجل' – the woman in all actions of Hajj is like the man. This establishes equality in pillars and essentials.
2
List the exceptions one by one
Work through each difference: (1) Head covering – she must cover her head (unlike man). (2) Veil – she drapes something over her face that does not touch it (recommended). (3) Talbiya – she does not raise her voice, just loud enough for herself to hear. (4) Ramal – she does not do ramal in tawaf. (5) Harwala – she does not run between green markers in sa'i. (6) Hair – she does not shave but only cuts (taqsir). (7) Clothing – she may wear sewn garments (makhit). (8) Stone kissing – she does not crowd men.
Each exception is based on modesty requirements or removal of hardship.
3
Understand the rationale
For each exception, note the reason: modesty (satr al-'awrah, avoiding fitna), weakness (not to be conspicuous or cause disruption). The veil is recommended to prevent contact of cloth with face.
The text mentions that the burqa' is disliked because it touches the face.
4
Identify the ruling level for each
Most exceptions are either mubah (permitted / not required) or sunna. For example, covering the head is wajib; not doing ramal is sunna (she is paused from a sunna); wearing sewn garments is mubah for women but haram for men.
Modifications are not punishments but accommodations.
5
Note omitted rulings
The author did not mention mahram requirement or exemption from farewell tawaf due to hayd. These are general rules not specific to Hajj rituals, hence omitted.
Do not confuse omission with denial; they are established elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج
كيفية تركيب أفعال الحج والمسمار الذي في وسط البيت يسمّونه سرّة الدنيا، يكشف أحدهم عورته وسرته ويضعها عليه فعل من لا عقل له فضلا عن علم كما قاله الكمال، وإذا أراد العود إلى أهله أن ابن همام أي الحاج ينبغي ينصرف بعد طوافه للوداع، وهو يمشي إلى ورائه ووجهه إلى البيت باكيا أو متباكيا متحسّرا على فراق البيت حتى يخرج من المسجد، ويخرج من حال من المستتر في "يمشى" مكة من باب بني شيبة من الثنية السفلى.
والمرأة في جميع أفعال الحج كالرجل غير أنها لا تكشف رأسها، وتسدل على وجهها شيئا تحته عيدان كالقبة تمنع مسه بالغطاء، ولا ترفع صوتها بالتلبية، ولا ترمل بل تسمع نفسها، ولا تهرول في السعي بين الميلين الأخضرين، بل تمشي على هينتها في جميع السعي بين الصفا والمروة، ولا تحلق، وتقصر وتلبس المخيط، ولا تزاحم الرجال في استلام الحجر، وهذا تمام حج المفرد، وهو دون المتمتع في الفضل والقرآن أفضل من التمتع.
والمسمار : لا يوجد هذا اللفظ في فتح القدير.
متباكيا: اسم الفاعل من التباكي ومعناه: التكلف بالبكاء.
لا تكشف رأسها والمراد بكشف الوجه عدم مماسة شيء له فلذا يكره لها أن تلبس البرقع؛ لأن ذلك يماس وجهها، كذا في "المبسوط" [البحر الرائق: ٥٤١/٢]
وتسدل وفي فتح القدير: إنه يستحب، مشتق من سدل بمعنى إرخاء الثوب من نصر. [البحر الرائق: ٥٤١/٢]
ولا ترمل أشار إلى أنها لا تضطبع؛ لأنه سنة الرمل وهي لا ترمل [البحر الرائق بحذف: ٥٤١/٢]
ولا تهرول: المضارع المنفي من الهرولة وهو نوع السير بين العدو والمشي وتقصر أفاد أنها كالرجل فيه، خلافا لما قيل: إنه لا يتقدر في حقها بالربع، بخلاف الرجل. [البحر الرائق: ٥٤١/٢]
وتلبس المخيط: وكذا تلبس الخفين والقفازين. [البحر الرائق: ٥٤١/٢]
ولا تزاحم إلخ فإن قلت لم ترك الشيخ أحكاما مخصوصة بها؟ منها أنها لا تحج إلا بمحرم بخلاف الرجل، ومنها أنها تترك طواف الصدر بعذر الحيض كما صرّح الشارح الزيلعي في شرحه للكنز، قلت لأن مثل هذه الأحكام ليس مما نحن فيه؛ لأن الأول لا يختص بالحج، بل هو حكم كل سفر، وكذا الثاني؛ لأن الحيض غير ممكن من الرجل حتى تخالفه في أحكامه.
Book of Hajj. How to compose the actions of Hajj and the nail that is in the middle of the House, they call it the navel of the world; one of them uncovers his private part and his navel and places it upon it, an act of one who has no intellect let alone knowledge, as al-Kamal said. And when he wishes to return to his family, Ibn Humam (i.e. the pilgrim) should depart after his farewell circumambulation, walking backwards with his face towards the House, crying or feigning crying, regretful over leaving the House, until he leaves the mosque. She does not shave her head, but she shortens, and she wears sewn garments, and she does not crowd men in kissing the Black Stone. I say: because such rulings are not what we are discussing; because the first is not specific to Hajj, rather it is a ruling for every journey, and likewise the second, because menstruation is not possible for the man such that he differs from her in its rulings.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement covering two aspects of ifrad Hajj: departure etiquette and women's specific rulings. The author begins by criticizing a superstitious practice involving a nail in the Kaaba, then describes the sunna manner of departing: walking backwards, weeping, and leaving from Bab Bani Shayba. The main body addresses women's rulings: they cover their heads, let down a veil that does not touch the face (recommended), do not raise their voice in talbiya, do not perform ramal or harwala in tawaf and sa'i, do not shave but only shorten hair, wear sewn garments, and do not crowd men when kissing the Black Stone. The commentary explains the dislike of the burqa', the recommendation of draping the veil, and that women's pace in sa'i is not measured like men's. It also justifies why certain gender-specific rulings (mahram, menstruation) are omitted here, as they are general travel or innate differences. The passage ends by noting the relative virtue of ifrad, tamattu', and qiran.

Key Terms
الترمل (al-ramal): Quick walking with short steps in the first three circuits of tawaf, a sunna for men only.
الهرولة (al-harwalah): A pace between walking and running, performed between the two green markers during sa'i; women do not do it.
المخيط (al-makhit): Sewn garments, which are normally prohibited for men in ihram but permitted for women.
التقصير (al-taqsir): Cutting a portion of hair after Hajj or Umrah; for women, it replaces shaving.

D) Shave her head.

Key Takeaway
Women's Hajj rulings are designed for modesty and ease; they differ from men in eight specific actions, but the core rituals remain the same.
